Here's what the search revealed:\n\n## Judge Evelyn Padin's AI Disclosure Requirements\n\nIn the District of New Jersey, Judge Evelyn Padin has proactively addressed generative AI in her courtroom procedures by amending her General Pretrial and Trial Procedures in late 2023 to require mandatory disclosure whenever attorneys use generative AI for court filings.\n\n### Specific Requirements\n\nThe use of any generative AI (such as OpenAI's ChatGPT or Google's Bard) in court filings requires a mandatory disclosure/certification that: (1) identifies the GAI program; (2) identifies the portion of the filing drafted by GAI; and (3) certifies that the GAI work product was diligently reviewed by a human being for accuracy and applicability.\n\n### Enforcement\n\nA failure to comply with this requirement may result in sanctions, such as the filing being stricken, and/or a referral to a bar association.\n\n### Philosophy\n\nJudge Padin's rule does not forbid using AI; rather, it insists on transparency and human oversight.\n\nThe procedures are available directly from the New Jersey District Court's website and apply to all matters pending before Judge Padin, including pro se litigants."}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"3deb1e97-d8dd-4b19-9a4c-4ebb0b92e983","uuid":"4e58f5dd-5473-43cb-85b6-533cf2b4b32e"}

Arteaga issued a May 15 update to policies requiring attorneys and self-represented litigants to identify AI-generated portions of documents, disclose tools used, and certify citation accuracy.\n\nJudge Stephen Alexander Vaden's order from June 8, 2023 requires submissions with AI-drafted text to include disclosure of the program used, specific portions drafted by AI, and certification that no confidential information was disclosed.\n\n## Repository Resources\n\nThe EDRM maintains a repository of judicial standing orders including AI segments that is current as of February 10, 2025, and Bloomberg Law offers a comparison table covering judicial standing orders on AI tools including ChatGPT, Google's BARD, and Anthropic's Claude.\n\n## Scope of Orders\n\nThe orders issued in 2025 are similar to those from 2023 and 2024, though some earlier orders went as far as banning AI from court documents. Let me provide you with a comprehensive summary of what I found:\n\n## Search Results Summary\n\nBased on my search, here's what I found regarding AI-related orders from federal district court judges in the Eastern, Western, and Middle Districts of Florida, Georgia, Alabama, and Louisiana during 2024-2025:\n\n### Alabama Federal District Courts\n\nIn the Northern District of Alabama, U.S. District Judge Anna Manasco imposed sanctions on three attorneys from Butler Snow LLP in July 2025 for submitting court filings containing fabricated legal citations generated by ChatGPT. The sanctioned attorneys—Matthew B. Reeves, William J. Cranford, and William R. Lunsford—were disqualified from further participation in the case and referred to the Alabama State Bar for disciplinary proceedings. Judge Manasco noted that monetary sanctions were apparently proving ineffective as a deterrent and instead disqualified the three Butler Snow partners from the case.\n\n### Louisiana Federal Districts and State Law\n\nOn August 1, 2025, Louisiana became the first state to establish a framework for addressing AI-generated evidence. Louisiana's Act No. 250 requires attorneys to \"exercise reasonable diligence to verify the authenticity of evidence before offering it to the court,\" and if an attorney \"knew or should have known through the exercise of reasonable diligence that evidence was false or artificially manipulated, the offering of that evidence without disclosure of that fact\" shall subject them to punishment for contempt of court. The Act further requires parties to disclose if any evidence has been \"generated by artificial intelligence or altered by any means\".\n\n### Florida State Courts (Not Federal)\n\nTwo of Florida's largest judicial circuits (state courts, not federal) now require lawyers and self-represented litigants to disclose AI-generated court submissions, with chief judges in Miami-Dade and Broward counties issuing AI-related administrative orders in recent weeks. Eleventh Judicial Circuit Chief Judge Ariana Fajardo Orshan issued Administrative Order No. 26-04 regarding disclosure of use of generative artificial intelligence on January 15, and Seventeenth Judicial Circuit Chief Judge Carol-Lisa Phillips issued Administrative Order 2026-03-Gen on January 26.\n\n### Georgia State Courts\n\nIn Georgia, an AI committee was established in August 2024, charged with assessing the risks and benefits associated with the use of Generative AI in the courts and making recommendations to help maintain public trust and confidence in the judicial system.\n\n### General Trends\n\nThere is no nationwide rule in 2025, and AI disclosure is a judge-by-judge, local-rule issue. Only two states, Hawaii and Nebraska, have AI disclosure required by the entire federal district.\n\n**Note:** My search did not reveal specific standing orders from individual federal district judges in the Eastern, Western, or Middle Districts of Florida, Georgia, or Louisiana requiring AI disclosure in court filings during 2024-2025, though the state-level developments in Louisiana and Florida state courts (not federal) are notable."}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"3deb1e97-d8dd-4b19-9a4c-4ebb0b92e983","uuid":"ca996261-ab34-4512-b95b-76ba16b05832"}

Here's what I found:\n\n## Overview\n\nSince Judge Brantley Starr of the U.S. District Court for the Northern District of Texas issued the first standing order on the use of AI in preparing court filings in 2023, hundreds of state and federal judges have amended or issued standing orders, general orders, local rules, pretrial orders, and other guidance to address AI use and misuse in their courtrooms.\n\n## Key Requirements\n\nThe answer about whether federal courts require AI disclosure in briefs changes by courtroom, not by circuit.(no content)Individual judges have issued standing orders or individual rules that, among other things, mandate disclosure of the use of AI when drafting/preparing submissions, require certification that any AI-generated document has been independently reviewed, and require counsel to confirm all filings comply with Rule 11, or its state court equivalent.\n\n## Notable Examples\n\n**Texas**: In mid-2023, U.S. District Judge Brantley Starr (N.D. Texas) issued a standing order directing attorneys to certify either that no generative AI was used in preparing filings or that any AI-generated content was thoroughly verified by a human.\n\n**Pennsylvania**: Judge Michael Baylson of the Eastern District of Pennsylvania issued a standing order requiring any attorney (or pro se party) appearing before him to affirmatively disclose AI use in the preparation of any filing. The order mandates \"a clear and plain factual statement\" in the document if an AI tool was used in any way, and further requires the lawyer to certify that every citation to law or the record has been verified for accuracy.\n\n**North Carolina**: The Charlotte Division's June 2024 standing order requires certification that either no generative AI was used (with exceptions for standard legal research platforms like Westlaw and Lexis) or that every statement and citation was verified by a human.\n\n## Current State\n\nDespite growing concern, there is not (yet) any single, district, or state-wide local rule on disclosing the use of AI in court filings.(no content)The rules vary dramatically across jurisdictions, leaving many practitioners uncertain about their obligations."}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"3deb1e97-d8dd-4b19-9a4c-4ebb0b92e983","uuid":"20d94cb6-eedb-40f4-b201-e27e4041a3b5"}

results, I found information about state court AI administrative orders in 2025-2026. Here's a summary:\n\n## Federal Executive Order (December 2025)\n\nOn December 11, 2025, President Trump signed an Executive Order titled \"Ensuring a National Policy Framework for Artificial Intelligence,\" which establishes a federal policy to \"sustain and enhance the United States' global AI dominance through a minimally burdensome national policy framework for AI,\" and outlines a series of steps to challenge or preempt state laws that conflict with that policy statement.(no content)The EO creates a new AI Litigation Task Force within the Department of Justice to identify and challenge state AI laws.\n\n## State Court AI Administrative Orders\n\n### Texas\n\n**Federal Courts:**\n- In mid-2023, U.S. District Judge Brantley Starr (N.D. Texas) issued a standing order directing attorneys to certify either that no generative AI was used in preparing filings or that any AI-generated content was thoroughly verified by a human.\n\n- On October 31, 2025, Chief Judge Mazzant issued General Order 25-07, which revised the Eastern District of Texas's Local Rule CV-11(g), requiring \"all litigants,\" not just pro se litigants, to review and verify the accuracy and quality of information produced by AI technology that is used in a filing.(no content)The revised rule is set to take effect on December 1, 2025, after a public notice and comment period.\n\n- At least one other federal district court in Texas has recently adopted a similar rule that requires the attorneys filing a brief to certify the use of generative AI technology on the first page of the brief (N.D. Tex. Loc. Civ. R. 7.2(f), effective Sept. 2, 2025).\n\n### Ohio\n\n**State Courts:**\n- In Ohio, courts are prohibited from using AI to translate legal forms, court orders and similar content that may affect the outcome of a case.\n\n- The Ohio Supreme Court has established an Artificial Intelligence Resource Library and references guidance documents including \"Artificial Intelligence Guidance for Use of AI and Generative AI in Courts\" from the National Center for State Courts, August 2024.\n\n- The Ohio Supreme Court adopted amendments to Court Interpreter Rules effective November 13, 2025, which introduce significant updates to regulate the use of artificial intelligence (AI) in interpretation and translation services.\n\n**Federal Courts:**\n- Judge Newman of the United States District Court of the Southern District of Ohio stipulates that \"[n]o attorney for a party, or a pro se party, may use Artificial Intelligence ('AI') in the preparation of any filing submitted to the Court.\"\n\n- Judge Boyko of the Northern District of Ohio has issued a standing order on the use of generative AI.\n\n### Michigan\n\n**State Courts:**\n- The Michigan Judicial Council (MJC) identified \"Generative Artificial Intelligence (GenAI) and the Courts\" as a key strategic initiative and established the GenAI and the Courts Workgroup in 2024 to study AI's impact on the judiciary.\n\n**Federal Courts:**\n- In late 2023, the Eastern District of Michigan proposed a formal local rule on AI disclosure. On December 8, 2023, the Eastern District's judges published a Notice of Proposed Amendments that included a new Local Rule 5.1(a)(4) titled \"Disclosing Use of Artificial Intelligence,\" which would mandate disclosure if generative AI is used to compose or draft any paper presented for filing, and require attestation that citations of authority have been verified by a human being.\n\n- On February 10, 2026, Magistrate Judge Anthony P. Patti of the U.S. District Court of the Eastern District for Michigan ruled in Warner v. Gilbarco that work product protection applied to materials generated by a third-party artificial intelligence tool prepared by a pro se plaintiff in the course of litigation.\n\n## State Legislation Activity\n\nIn 2025, over 40 states introduced about 250 bills related to government use of AI. The states include Kansas, Montana, Delaware, New Mexico, Illinois, Iowa, Maryland, Michigan, Ohio, Rhode Island, Connecticut and Tennessee."}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"3deb1e97-d8dd-4b19-9a4c-4ebb0b92e983","uuid":"59210374-b3a4-4a7f-bb9e-ba83623e491d"}
